Problem: Determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. A curve is called an osculating curve if its position vector lies on its osculating plane. The osculating plane for the curve \( \alpha(s) \) at some point is generated by the tangent vector and normal vector of \( \alpha \) at that point, i.e., \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)+\lambda_2(s)n(s) \) for some functions \( \lambda_1(s) \) and \( \lambda_2(s) \). Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Let \( \alpha(s) \) be a non-trivial osculating curve on the Euclidean space. We know that \[ T(s) = \lambda_1(s) t(s)+\lambda_2(s) \] where \( t(s) \) is the tangent vector of \( \alpha \) and \( n(s) \) is its normal vector. Rearranging this equation, we get \[ \alpha(s)= \lambda_1(s)t(s) +\lambda_2(s)n(s)+\alpha(s)' \] Since this is an osculating curve, by definition it means \( \alpha(s)= \lambda_1(s)t(s) +\lambda_2(s)n(s) \), so \alpha(s)' = 0. Since this is true for any \( s \), we have \[v(s)=\lambda_2(s t(s)-n(s))=0 \] for all \( s \), so \( v(s)=\lambda_1(s)=0 \). Therefore, only \( \lambda_2(s) \) is nonzero, so \( T(s) = \lambda_1(s)d_z + 0\cdot d_x + 0\cdot d_y \). \boxed{No}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. Let me start by recalling some basic concepts. An osculating curve is defined as a curve whose position vector lies entirely within its osculating plane. The osculating plane at a point on a curve is the plane spanned by the tangent vector and the normal vector. So, for such a curve, we can write the position vector α(s) as a linear combination of the tangent vector t(s) and the normal vector n(s), right? That is, α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s) for some scalar functions λ₁ and λ₂. Now, the question is about torsion. Torsion τ measures how much the curve twists out of the osculating plane. If the torsion is zero, the curve lies entirely within a fixed plane, making it a plane curve. So, intuitively, if the position vector is always in the osculating plane, maybe the curve doesn't twist out of that plane, implying zero torsion. But I need to verify this mathematically. Let me recall the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length s: t'(s) = κ(s)n(s) n'(s) = -κ(s)t(s) + τ(s)b(s) b'(s) = -τ(s)n(s) Here, t is the tangent vector, n is the normal vector, b is the binormal vector, κ is the curvature, and τ is the torsion. Since the position vector α(s) lies in the osculating plane, it can be expressed as α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s). Let me differentiate this expression with respect to s to see if I can derive relations involving κ and τ. First, compute α'(s). Since α is parameterized by arc length, α'(s) = t(s). Differentiating the given expression: α'(s) = d/ds [λ₁ t + λ₂ n] = λ₁' t + λ₁ t' + λ₂' n + λ₂ n' Using the Frenet-Serret formulas, substitute t' = κ n and n' = -κ t + τ b: = λ₁' t + λ₁ (κ n) + λ₂' n + λ₂ (-κ t + τ b) = (λ₁' - κ λ₂) t + (λ₁ κ + λ₂') n + (λ₂ τ) b But we know that α'(s) = t(s). Therefore, equating components: 1. The coefficient of t: (λ₁' - κ λ₂) = 1 2. The coefficient of n: (λ₁ κ + λ₂') = 0 3. The coefficient of b: λ₂ τ = 0 So, from the third equation, we have λ₂ τ = 0. This equation must hold for all s. If λ₂ is not identically zero, then τ must be zero. However, if λ₂ is zero everywhere, then from the second equation, λ₁ κ = 0. If κ is not zero (since we are dealing with a curve with an osculating plane, curvature can't be zero?), then λ₁ must be zero. But then from the first equation, 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, λ₂ cannot be identically zero. Therefore, since λ₂ is not identically zero, from λ₂ τ = 0, it follows that τ must be identically zero. Hence, the torsion is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, let me check this again. Suppose λ₂ is not zero at some point, then τ must be zero at that point. But since we have to hold for all s, unless λ₂ is zero everywhere, which we saw leads to a contradiction, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion is zero. Alternatively, maybe there's a case where λ₂ is zero at some points but not others. For example, if λ₂ is zero in some intervals and non-zero in others. But then in the intervals where λ₂ is non-zero, τ would have to be zero. However, torsion is a smooth function, so if τ is zero on an interval, and the curve is analytic, it would be zero everywhere. But the problem doesn't specify analyticity, so torsion could be zero on some parts and non-zero on others where λ₂ is zero. However, in the regions where λ₂ is zero, from the second equation, λ₁ κ + λ₂' = 0. If λ₂ is zero in an interval, then λ₂' is also zero there, so λ₁ κ = 0. If κ ≠ 0, then λ₁ = 0. Then from the first equation, λ₁' - κ λ₂ = 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, even if λ₂ is zero in an interval, we get a contradiction, so λ₂ cannot be zero in any interval. Therefore, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion τ is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, but let me think of an example. A plane curve has τ = 0, and its position vector lies in the plane, which is the osculating plane. So, yes, plane curves are osculating curves with τ = 0. But are there non-plane curves (with τ ≠ 0) that are osculating curves? According to the above reasoning, no. Because if the position vector lies in the osculating plane everywhere, then τ must be zero. Alternatively, maybe a circular helix? But the position vector of a helix doesn't lie in the osculating plane. Let me check. The osculating plane of a helix is the plane spanned by t and n. The position vector for a helix is (a cos t, a sin t, bt). The tangent vector t is (-a sin t, a cos t, b)/sqrt(a² + b²), and the normal vector n is (-cos t, -sin t, 0). So, the osculating plane is spanned by t and n. The position vector would have components in the t, n, and b directions? Maybe not lying entirely in the osculating plane. So probably, helices are not osculating curves. Therefore, the conclusion is that torsion must be zero. Therefore, the answer is yes, the torsion is zero for such a curve. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space, we start by noting that an osculating curve has its position vector lying in the osculating plane. This means the position vector \(\alpha(s)\) can be expressed as \(\alpha(s) = \lambda_1(s)t(s) + \lambda_2(s)n(s)\) for some scalar functions \(\lambda_1(s)\) and \(\lambda_2(s)\). Using the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length \(s\): \[ \begin{aligned} t'(s) &= \kappa(s)n(s), \\ n'(s) &= -\kappa(s)t(s) + \tau(s)b(s), \\ b'(s) &= -\tau(s)n(s), \end{aligned} \] we differentiate the position vector \(\alpha(s)\): \[ \alpha'(s) = \frac{d}{ds}[\lambda_1 t + \lambda_2 n] = \lambda_1' t + \lambda_1 t' + \lambda_2' n + \lambda_2 n'. \] Substituting \(t' = \kappa n\) and \(n' = -\kappa t + \tau b\), we get: \[ \alpha'(s) = (\lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2) t + (\lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2') n + (\lambda_2 \tau) b. \] Since \(\alpha'(s) = t(s)\), equating the components of the tangent, normal, and binormal vectors gives: \[ \begin{aligned} 1. &\quad \lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2 = 1, \\ 2. &\quad \lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2' = 0, \\ 3. &\quad \lambda_2 \tau = 0. \end{aligned} \] From the third equation, \(\lambda_2 \tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 \neq 0\), then \(\tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 = 0\) in some interval, the second equation implies \(\lambda_1 \kappa = 0\). If \(\kappa \neq 0\), then \(\lambda_1 = 0\), leading to a contradiction in the first equation. Thus, \(\lambda_2\) cannot be zero in any interval, implying \(\tau = 0\) everywhere. Therefore, the torsion \(\tau\) must be zero for an osculating curve. \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. A curve is called an osculating curve if its position vector lies on its osculating plane. Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: If the osculating plane of a curve at a point \( s \) is just the tangent plane of a nearby plane curve \( \gamma \), then the curve is \( \alpha(s)=\gamma(s) \). \boxed{Yes}

This equation must hold for all s. If λ₂ is not identically zero, then τ must be zero. However, if λ₂ is zero everywhere, then from the second equation, λ₁ κ = 0. If κ is not zero (since we are dealing with a curve with an osculating plane, curvature can't be zero?), then λ₁ must be zero. But then from the first equation, 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, λ₂ cannot be identically zero. Therefore, since λ₂ is not identically zero, from λ₂ τ = 0, it follows that τ must be identically zero. Hence, the torsion is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, let me check this again. Suppose λ₂ is not zero at some point, then τ must be zero at that point. But since we have to hold for all s, unless λ₂ is zero everywhere, which we saw leads to a contradiction, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion is zero. Alternatively, maybe there's a case where λ₂ is zero at some points but not others. For example, if λ₂ is zero in some intervals and non-zero in others. But then in the intervals where λ₂ is non-zero, τ would have to be zero. However, torsion is a smooth function, so if τ is zero on an interval, and the curve is analytic, it would be zero everywhere. But the problem doesn't specify analyticity, so torsion could be zero on some parts and non-zero on others where λ₂ is zero. However, in the regions where λ₂ is zero, from the second equation, λ₁ κ + λ₂' = 0. If λ₂ is zero in an interval, then λ₂' is also zero there, so λ₁ κ = 0. If κ ≠ 0, then λ₁ = 0. Then from the first equation, λ₁' - κ λ₂ = 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, even if λ₂ is zero in an interval, we get a contradiction, so λ₂ cannot be zero in any interval. Therefore, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion τ is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, but let me think of an example. A plane curve has τ = 0, and its position vector lies in the plane, which is the osculating plane. So, yes, plane curves are osculating curves with τ = 0. But are there non-plane curves (with τ ≠ 0) that are osculating curves? According to the above reasoning, no. Because if the position vector lies in the osculating plane everywhere, then τ must be zero. Alternatively, maybe a circular helix? So, the osculating plane is spanned by t and n. The position vector would have components in the t, n, and b directions? Maybe not lying entirely in the osculating plane. So probably, helices are not osculating curves. Therefore, the conclusion is that torsion must be zero. Therefore, the answer is yes, the torsion is zero for such a curve. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space, we start by noting that an osculating curve has its position vector lying in the osculating plane. This means the position vector \(\alpha(s)\) can be expressed as \(\alpha(s) = \lambda_1(s)t(s) + \lambda_2(s)n(s)\) for some scalar functions \(\lambda_1(s)\) and \(\lambda_2(s)\). Using the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length \(s\): \[ \begin{aligned} t'(s) &= \kappa(s)n(s), \\ n'(s) &= -\kappa(s)t(s) + \tau(s)b(s), \\ b'(s) &= -\tau(s)n(s), \end{aligned} \] we differentiate the position vector \(\alpha(s)\): \[ \alpha'(s) = \frac{d}{ds}[\lambda_1 t + \lambda_2 n] = \lambda_1' t + \lambda_1 t' + \lambda_2' n + \lambda_2 n'. \] Substituting \(t' = \kappa n\) and \(n' = -\kappa t + \tau b\), we get: \[ \alpha'(s) = (\lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2) t + (\lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2') n + (\lambda_2 \tau) b. \] Since \(\alpha'(s) = t(s)\), equating the components of the tangent, normal, and binormal vectors gives: \[ \begin{aligned} 1. &\quad \lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2 = 1, \\ 2. &\quad \lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2' = 0, \\ 3. &\quad \lambda_2 \tau = 0. \end{aligned} \] From the third equation, \(\lambda_2 \tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 \neq 0\), then \(\tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 = 0\) in some interval, the second equation implies \(\lambda_1 \kappa = 0\). If \(\kappa \neq 0\), then \(\lambda_1 = 0\), leading to a contradiction in the first equation. Thus, \(\lambda_2\) cannot be zero in any interval, implying \(\tau = 0\) everywhere. Therefore, the torsion \(\tau\) must be zero for an osculating curve. \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. A curve is called an osculating curve if its position vector lies on its osculating plane. The osculating plane for the curve \( \alpha(s) \) at some point is generated by the tangent vector and normal vector of \( \alpha \) at that point, i.e., \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)+\lambda_2(s)n(s) \) for some functions \( \lambda_1(s) \) and \( \lambda_2(s) \). Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Consider the osculating curve at some point \( P=\alpha(s_0) \). Let the position vector be denoted by \( \label{asn11} \alpha'= \lambda_1(s)t+\lambda_2(s)n \). Take the derivative of this expression with respect to \( s \) to find \( \alpha''=\lambda_1''t+\lambda_1'nt+\lambda_2''n+\lambda_2'n=\alpha'-ta \). Therefore, \( \lambda_1'=n \cdot a=a_\perp \) and \( \lambda_2=n-u \cdot a \). We can use \( \lambda_2=0 \) to eliminate one of the functions. All that is left is \( \lambda_1'=a_\perp \). Therefore, \boxed{$$T=0$$}

Problem: Determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. A curve is called an osculating curve if its position vector lies on its osculating plane. The osculating plane for the curve \( \alpha(s) \) at some point is generated by the tangent vector and normal vector of \( \alpha \) at that point, i.e., \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)+\lambda_2(s)n(s) \) for some functions \( \lambda_1(s) \) and \( \lambda_2(s) \). Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: A vector r is tangent if and only if it is aligned with the first factor r_1, and it is normal if it is perpendicular to both r_1 and r_2, i.e. it lies in the space perpendicular to r_1 and r_2. For continuous \( \lambda_1(s) \) and \( \lambda_2(s) \) we can apply the implicit function theorem at this point to one of the vectors \( \lambda_1(s)t(s) \) and one of the vectors \( \lambda_2(s)n(s) \). If the implicit function does not blow up in this step, we have $$ \alpha(s)=g\left(\lambda_1(s)t(s),\lambda_2(s)n(s)\right) $$ with some function \( g(x,y) \), where \( n \) is given by $$ n(s)=r_2(s)-\left\langle r_1(s),r_2(s)\right\rangle r_1(s) $$ The function \( g \) determines a torus because the quotient space \( g^{-1}\left(G\right)/G' \) is constant, with \( G=\left\langle r_1(s),r_2(s)\right\rangle \) and \( G'=\left\langle n(s)\right\rangle \). \boxed{yes}<|end_of_text|>

Then from the first equation, λ₁' - κ λ₂ = 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, even if λ₂ is zero in an interval, we get a contradiction, so λ₂ cannot be zero in any interval. Therefore, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion τ is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, but let me think of an example. A plane curve has τ = 0, and its position vector lies in the plane, which is the osculating plane. So, yes, plane curves are osculating curves with τ = 0. But are there non-plane curves (with τ ≠ 0) that are osculating curves? According to the above reasoning, no. Because if the position vector lies in the osculating plane everywhere, then τ must be zero. Alternatively, maybe a circular helix? But the position vector of a helix doesn't lie in the osculating plane. Let me check. The osculating plane of a helix is the plane spanned by t and n. The position vector for a helix is (a cos t, a sin t, bt). The tangent vector t is (-a sin t, a cos t, b)/sqrt(a² + b²), and the normal vector n is (-cos t, -sin t, 0). So, the osculating plane is spanned by t and n. The position vector would have components in the t, n, and b directions? Maybe not lying entirely in the osculating plane. This means the position vector \(\alpha(s)\) can be expressed as \(\alpha(s) = \lambda_1(s)t(s) + \lambda_2(s)n(s)\) for some scalar functions \(\lambda_1(s)\) and \(\lambda_2(s)\). Using the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length \(s\): \[ \begin{aligned} t'(s) &= \kappa(s)n(s), \\ n'(s) &= -\kappa(s)t(s) + \tau(s)b(s), \\ b'(s) &= -\tau(s)n(s), \end{aligned} \] we differentiate the position vector \(\alpha(s)\): \[ \alpha'(s) = \frac{d}{ds}[\lambda_1 t + \lambda_2 n] = \lambda_1' t + \lambda_1 t' + \lambda_2' n + \lambda_2 n'. \] Substituting \(t' = \kappa n\) and \(n' = -\kappa t + \tau b\), we get: \[ \alpha'(s) = (\lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2) t + (\lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2') n + (\lambda_2 \tau) b. \] Since \(\alpha'(s) = t(s)\), equating the components of the tangent, normal, and binormal vectors gives: \[ \begin{aligned} 1. &\quad \lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2 = 1, \\ 2. &\quad \lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2' = 0, \\ 3. &\quad \lambda_2 \tau = 0. \end{aligned} \] From the third equation, \(\lambda_2 \tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 \neq 0\), then \(\tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 = 0\) in some interval, the second equation implies \(\lambda_1 \kappa = 0\). If \(\kappa \neq 0\), then \(\lambda_1 = 0\), leading to a contradiction in the first equation. Thus, \(\lambda_2\) cannot be zero in any interval, implying \(\tau = 0\) everywhere. Therefore, the torsion \(\tau\) must be zero for an osculating curve. \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. Let me start by recalling some basic concepts. An osculating curve is defined as a curve whose position vector lies entirely within its osculating plane. The osculating plane at a point on a curve is the plane spanned by the tangent vector and the normal vector. So, for such a curve, we can write the position vector α(s) as a linear combination of the tangent vector t(s) and the normal vector n(s), right? That is, α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s) for some scalar functions λ₁ and λ₂. Now, the question is about torsion. Torsion τ measures how much the curve twists out of the osculating plane. If the torsion is zero, the curve lies entirely within a fixed plane, making it a plane curve. So, intuitively, if the position vector is always in the osculating plane, maybe the curve doesn't twist out of that plane, implying zero torsion. But I need to verify this mathematically. Let me recall the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length s: t'(s) = κ(s)n(s) n'(s) = -κ(s)t(s) + τ(s)b(s) b'(s) = -τ(s)n(s) Here, t is the tangent vector, n is the normal vector, b is the binormal vector, κ is the curvature, and τ is the torsion. Since the position vector α(s) lies in the osculating plane, it can be expressed as α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s). Let me differentiate this expression with respect to s to see if I can derive relations involving κ and τ. First, compute α'(s). Since α is parameterized by arc length, α'(s) = t(s). Problem: Determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. A curve is called an osculating curve if its position vector lies on its osculating plane. The osculating plane for the curve \( \alpha(s) \) at some point is generated by the tangent vector and normal vector of \( \alpha \) at that point, i.e., \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)+\lambda_2(s)n(s) \) for some functions \( \lambda_1(s) \) and \( \lambda_2(s) \). Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes, the torsion is zero. Let \( \lambda_1(s) \) be the Euclidean length of the tangent vector and let \( \lambda_2(s) \) be its dot product with our base vector \( e_{1} \). Then its osculating curve can be written \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)=\lambda_1(s)e_{1}t(s)+\lambda_2(s)e_{2}t(s) \). This differs from the definition of \( \alpha(s) \) by the first coordinate, which is zero. Therefore, the osculating plane is just an element of the family \( r_{1}e_{1}+r_{2}e_{2} \) of planes that keep the base vector \( e_{1} \). If there exists a point \( x \in \mathbb{E}^{3} \) such that \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s) \) for some \( s \), then \( x=r_{1}e_{1} \), so, if the \( e_{1} \)-coordinate of \( x \) is nonzero, the osculating plane reduces to \( e_{1} \), and \( \lambda_1(s)=1 \), so we can write \( x=\mu t(s) \) for some function \( \mu(s) \), contradicting the definition of \( \alpha(s) \). Thus \( \lambda_1(s)=0 \) for all \( s \), and \( \lambda_2(s)=0 \) for all \( s \), so \( \tfrac{\partial\alpha}{\partial s}=\lambda_1\left(1t+w=\alpha=0t+w=0\right)=e_{2}n(s) \), where \( n(s)=t(s)\times w(s) \) is a normal vector, and \( \lambda_1(t)=0 \) for all \( t \), so \( \lVert n \rVert=\lVert n \rVert \) at all times, which means \( n(s)\) is constant, so \( n(s)=n \) for all \( s \), and \( t(s)\times n \) is also a normal vector, which means \( n(t)\times n=s \) for all \( t \), so, since \( n\neq 0 \) at some \( t_{0} \), we must have \( \lambda_{2}(t_{0})=s_{1}=0 \), at all \( t \), so \( n(t)=n \) for all \( t \). Thus, our curve \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)+\lambda_2(s)n \) has a constant normal, which we can re-write as a composition of functions \( \alpha(s)=\lambda\left(\lambda_{1}(s)n\right)+\lambda_2(s)n \) where \( \lambda \) is a scalar function at all \( s \). Taking the derivative we see that \( \tfrac{d\alpha}{d\tau}=\tfrac{\partial\alpha}{\partial\lambda_{1}}\tfrac{d \lambda_{1}}{ds}+\tfrac{\partial\alpha}{\partial\lambda_{2}}\tfrac{d \lambda_{2}}{ds} \), so the torsion is zero. \boxed{Yes}<|end_of_text|>

Response: The curvature is given by \( k(s)=\frac{|t(s)\times n(s)|}{|\alpha(s)|^3} \) and the torsion is defined as \( T(s)=\frac{k(s)'}{|\alpha(s)|}\). The numerator parametrically vanishes only when \( \alpha \) lies in the osculating plane, but we can see that the denominator always vanishes. Therefore, the torsion is never zero. \boxed{NO}

So, the osculating plane is spanned by t and n. The position vector would have components in the t, n, and b directions? Maybe not lying entirely in the osculating plane. So probably, helices are not osculating curves. Therefore, the conclusion is that torsion must be zero. Therefore, the answer is yes, the torsion is zero for such a curve. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space, we start by noting that an osculating curve has its position vector lying in the osculating plane. This means the position vector \(\alpha(s)\) can be expressed as \(\alpha(s) = \lambda_1(s)t(s) + \lambda_2(s)n(s)\) for some scalar functions \(\lambda_1(s)\) and \(\lambda_2(s)\). Using the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length \(s\): \[ \begin{aligned} t'(s) &= \kappa(s)n(s), \\ n'(s) &= -\kappa(s)t(s) + \tau(s)b(s), \\ b'(s) &= -\tau(s)n(s), \end{aligned} \] we differentiate the position vector \(\alpha(s)\): \[ \alpha'(s) = \frac{d}{ds}[\lambda_1 t + \lambda_2 n] = \lambda_1' t + \lambda_1 t' + \lambda_2' n + \lambda_2 n'. \] Substituting \(t' = \kappa n\) and \(n' = -\kappa t + \tau b\), we get: \[ \alpha'(s) = (\lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2) t + (\lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2') n + (\lambda_2 \tau) b. \] Since \(\alpha'(s) = t(s)\), equating the components of the tangent, normal, and binormal vectors gives: \[ \begin{aligned} 1. &\quad \lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2 = 1, \\ 2. &\quad \lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2' = 0, \\ 3. &\quad \lambda_2 \tau = 0. \end{aligned} \] From the third equation, \(\lambda_2 \tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 \neq 0\), then \(\tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 = 0\) in some interval, the second equation implies \(\lambda_1 \kappa = 0\). If \(\kappa \neq 0\), then \(\lambda_1 = 0\), leading to a contradiction in the first equation. Thus, \(\lambda_2\) cannot be zero in any interval, implying \(\tau = 0\) everywhere. Therefore, the torsion \(\tau\) must be zero for an osculating curve. \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. A curve is called an osculating curve if its position vector lies on its osculating plane. The osculating plane for the curve \( \alpha(s) \) at some point is generated by the tangent vector and normal vector of \( \alpha \) at that point, i.e., \( \alpha(s)=\lambda_1(s)t(s)+\lambda_2(s)n(s) \) for some functions \( \lambda_1(s) \) and \( \lambda_2(s) \). Is the torsion zero for such a curve? Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Yes. The osculating plane for any curve lies within the tangent plane through any point on the curve. \boxed{Yes}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to figure out if the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space. Let me start by recalling some basic concepts. An osculating curve is defined as a curve whose position vector lies entirely within its osculating plane. The osculating plane at a point on a curve is the plane spanned by the tangent vector and the normal vector. So, for such a curve, we can write the position vector α(s) as a linear combination of the tangent vector t(s) and the normal vector n(s), right? That is, α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s) for some scalar functions λ₁ and λ₂. Now, the question is about torsion. Torsion τ measures how much the curve twists out of the osculating plane. If the torsion is zero, the curve lies entirely within a fixed plane, making it a plane curve. So, intuitively, if the position vector is always in the osculating plane, maybe the curve doesn't twist out of that plane, implying zero torsion. But I need to verify this mathematically. Let me recall the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length s: t'(s) = κ(s)n(s) n'(s) = -κ(s)t(s) + τ(s)b(s) b'(s) = -τ(s)n(s) Here, t is the tangent vector, n is the normal vector, b is the binormal vector, κ is the curvature, and τ is the torsion. Since the position vector α(s) lies in the osculating plane, it can be expressed as α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s). Let me differentiate this expression with respect to s to see if I can derive relations involving κ and τ. First, compute α'(s). Since α is parameterized by arc length, α'(s) = t(s). Differentiating the given expression: α'(s) = d/ds [λ₁ t + λ₂ n] = λ₁' t + λ₁ t' + λ₂' n + λ₂ n' Using the Frenet-Serret formulas, substitute t' = κ n and n' = -κ t + τ b: = λ₁' t + λ₁ (κ n) + λ₂' n + λ₂ (-κ t + τ b) = (λ₁' - κ λ₂) t + (λ₁ κ + λ₂') n + (λ₂ τ) b But we know that α'(s) = t(s). Therefore, equating components: 1. The coefficient of t: (λ₁' - κ λ₂) = 1 2. The coefficient of n: (λ₁ κ + λ₂') = 0 3. The coefficient of b: λ₂ τ = 0 So, from the third equation, we have λ₂ τ = 0. This equation must hold for all s. If λ₂ is not identically zero, then τ must be zero. However, if λ₂ is zero everywhere, then from the second equation, λ₁ κ = 0. If κ is not zero (since we are dealing with a curve with an osculating plane, curvature can't be zero?), then λ₁ must be zero. But then from the first equation, 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, λ₂ cannot be identically zero. Therefore, since λ₂ is not identically zero, from λ₂ τ = 0, it follows that τ must be identically zero. Hence, the torsion is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, let me check this again. Suppose λ₂ is not zero at some point, then τ must be zero at that point. But since we have to hold for all s, unless λ₂ is zero everywhere, which we saw leads to a contradiction, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion is zero. Alternatively, maybe there's a case where λ₂ is zero at some points but not others. For example, if λ₂ is zero in some intervals and non-zero in others. But then in the intervals where λ₂ is non-zero, τ would have to be zero. However, torsion is a smooth function, so if τ is zero on an interval, and the curve is analytic, it would be zero everywhere. But the problem doesn't specify analyticity, so torsion could be zero on some parts and non-zero on others where λ₂ is zero. However, in the regions where λ₂ is zero, from the second equation, λ₁ κ + λ₂' = 0. If λ₂ is zero in an interval, then λ₂' is also zero there, so λ₁ κ = 0. If κ ≠ 0, then λ₁ = 0. Then from the first equation, λ₁' - κ λ₂ = 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, even if λ₂ is zero in an interval, we get a contradiction, so λ₂ cannot be zero in any interval. Therefore, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion τ is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, but let me think of an example. A plane curve has τ = 0, and its position vector lies in the plane, which is the osculating plane. So, yes, plane curves are osculating curves with τ = 0. That is, α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s) for some scalar functions λ₁ and λ₂. Now, the question is about torsion. Torsion τ measures how much the curve twists out of the osculating plane. If the torsion is zero, the curve lies entirely within a fixed plane, making it a plane curve. So, intuitively, if the position vector is always in the osculating plane, maybe the curve doesn't twist out of that plane, implying zero torsion. But I need to verify this mathematically. Let me recall the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length s: t'(s) = κ(s)n(s) n'(s) = -κ(s)t(s) + τ(s)b(s) b'(s) = -τ(s)n(s) Here, t is the tangent vector, n is the normal vector, b is the binormal vector, κ is the curvature, and τ is the torsion. Since the position vector α(s) lies in the osculating plane, it can be expressed as α(s) = λ₁(s)t(s) + λ₂(s)n(s). Let me differentiate this expression with respect to s to see if I can derive relations involving κ and τ. First, compute α'(s). Since α is parameterized by arc length, α'(s) = t(s). Differentiating the given expression: α'(s) = d/ds [λ₁ t + λ₂ n] = λ₁' t + λ₁ t' + λ₂' n + λ₂ n' Using the Frenet-Serret formulas, substitute t' = κ n and n' = -κ t + τ b: = λ₁' t + λ₁ (κ n) + λ₂' n + λ₂ (-κ t + τ b) = (λ₁' - κ λ₂) t + (λ₁ κ + λ₂') n + (λ₂ τ) b But we know that α'(s) = t(s). Therefore, equating components: 1. The coefficient of t: (λ₁' - κ λ₂) = 1 2. The coefficient of n: (λ₁ κ + λ₂') = 0 3. The coefficient of b: λ₂ τ = 0 So, from the third equation, we have λ₂ τ = 0. This equation must hold for all s. If λ₂ is not identically zero, then τ must be zero. However, if λ₂ is zero everywhere, then from the second equation, λ₁ κ = 0. If κ is not zero (since we are dealing with a curve with an osculating plane, curvature can't be zero?), then λ₁ must be zero. But then from the first equation, 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, λ₂ cannot be identically zero. Therefore, since λ₂ is not identically zero, from λ₂ τ = 0, it follows that τ must be identically zero. Hence, the torsion is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, let me check this again. Suppose λ₂ is not zero at some point, then τ must be zero at that point. But since we have to hold for all s, unless λ₂ is zero everywhere, which we saw leads to a contradiction, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion is zero. Alternatively, maybe there's a case where λ₂ is zero at some points but not others. For example, if λ₂ is zero in some intervals and non-zero in others. But then in the intervals where λ₂ is non-zero, τ would have to be zero. However, torsion is a smooth function, so if τ is zero on an interval, and the curve is analytic, it would be zero everywhere. But the problem doesn't specify analyticity, so torsion could be zero on some parts and non-zero on others where λ₂ is zero. However, in the regions where λ₂ is zero, from the second equation, λ₁ κ + λ₂' = 0. If λ₂ is zero in an interval, then λ₂' is also zero there, so λ₁ κ = 0. If κ ≠ 0, then λ₁ = 0. Then from the first equation, λ₁' - κ λ₂ = 0 - 0 = 1, which is impossible. Therefore, even if λ₂ is zero in an interval, we get a contradiction, so λ₂ cannot be zero in any interval. Therefore, τ must be zero everywhere. Therefore, the torsion τ is zero for an osculating curve. Wait, but let me think of an example. A plane curve has τ = 0, and its position vector lies in the plane, which is the osculating plane. So, yes, plane curves are osculating curves with τ = 0. So probably, helices are not osculating curves. Therefore, the conclusion is that torsion must be zero. Therefore, the answer is yes, the torsion is zero for such a curve. **Final Answer** \boxed{Yes} </think> To determine whether the torsion is zero for an osculating curve in Euclidean space, we start by noting that an osculating curve has its position vector lying in the osculating plane. This means the position vector \(\alpha(s)\) can be expressed as \(\alpha(s) = \lambda_1(s)t(s) + \lambda_2(s)n(s)\) for some scalar functions \(\lambda_1(s)\) and \(\lambda_2(s)\). Using the Frenet-Serret formulas for a curve parameterized by arc length \(s\): \[ \begin{aligned} t'(s) &= \kappa(s)n(s), \\ n'(s) &= -\kappa(s)t(s) + \tau(s)b(s), \\ b'(s) &= -\tau(s)n(s), \end{aligned} \] we differentiate the position vector \(\alpha(s)\): \[ \alpha'(s) = \frac{d}{ds}[\lambda_1 t + \lambda_2 n] = \lambda_1' t + \lambda_1 t' + \lambda_2' n + \lambda_2 n'. \] Substituting \(t' = \kappa n\) and \(n' = -\kappa t + \tau b\), we get: \[ \alpha'(s) = (\lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2) t + (\lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2') n + (\lambda_2 \tau) b. \] Since \(\alpha'(s) = t(s)\), equating the components of the tangent, normal, and binormal vectors gives: \[ \begin{aligned} 1. &\quad \lambda_1' - \kappa \lambda_2 = 1, \\ 2. &\quad \lambda_1 \kappa + \lambda_2' = 0, \\ 3. &\quad \lambda_2 \tau = 0. \end{aligned} \] From the third equation, \(\lambda_2 \tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 \neq 0\), then \(\tau = 0\). If \(\lambda_2 = 0\) in some interval, the second equation implies \(\lambda_1 \kappa = 0\). If \(\kappa \neq 0\), then \(\lambda_1 = 0\), leading to a contradiction in the first equation. Thus, \(\lambda_2\) cannot be zero in any interval, implying \(\tau = 0\) everywhere. Therefore, the torsion \(\tau\) must be zero for an osculating curve. \[ \boxed{Yes} \]

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Use integration by parts to compute the \(\int \sin{u} \cdot u^{\prime} \ \mathrm{d}u = - \cos{u}. \boxed{0} \) Back to All Questions<|end_of_text|>

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this improper integral: \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \). Hmm, let's see. First, this looks a bit intimidating because of the combination of cosine and sine functions with the square root in the denominator. But maybe there's a substitution or a trick that can simplify this. Let me start by analyzing the integrand. The denominator is \(\sqrt{1 + x^2}\), which reminds me of hyperbolic identities because if I set \(x = \sinh t\), then \(\sqrt{1 + x^2} = \cosh t\), and \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\). Maybe that substitution would help here. Let me try that. Let’s set \(x = \sinh t\). Then, \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\), and when \(x = 0\), \(t = 0\), and as \(x \to \infty\), \(t \to \infty\). Substituting into the integral, the denominator becomes \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), so the denominator cancels out with the \(\cosh t\) from the substitution. That simplifies the denominator part. So the integral becomes: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\cos(\sinh t) \sin(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t})}{\cosh t} \cdot \cosh t \, dt = \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Wait, because \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), right? So the sine term becomes \(\sin(\cosh t)\). So the integral simplifies to: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Hmm, that seems a bit simpler, but still not straightforward. Maybe there's a trigonometric identity I can use here. The product of cosine and sine can be written as a sum of sines: \[ \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \] Let me apply that identity here with \(A = \sinh t\) and \(B = \cosh t\). Then: \[ \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(\cosh t + \sinh t) + \sin(\cosh t - \sinh t)] \] Now, \(\cosh t + \sinh t = e^t\) because \(\cosh t = \frac{e^t + e^{-t}}{2}\) and \(\sinh t = \frac{e^t - e^{-t}}{2}\), so adding them gives \(e^t\). Similarly, \(\cosh t - \sinh t = e^{-t}\). So the expression becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \, dt \] So now, I need to evaluate two integrals: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) Let me handle them one by one. Starting with the second integral because it might be simpler. Let’s make a substitution for the second integral: Let \(u = e^{-t}\). Then, \(du = -e^{-t} dt\), which implies \(dt = -\frac{du}{u}\). When \(t = 0\), \(u = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(u \to 0\). Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{0} \sin(u) \left(-\frac{du}{u}\right) = \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] Oh, that's a familiar integral. The integral of \(\frac{\sin u}{u}\) from 0 to 1 is known as the sine integral function, denoted \(\text{Si}(1)\). But in general, \(\int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du\) doesn't have an elementary closed-form, but maybe we can leave it as \(\text{Si}(1)\) for now. Wait, but perhaps when combined with the first integral, something cancels or simplifies. Now, let's look at the first integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \). Let me make a substitution here as well. Let \(v = e^t\), so that \(dv = e^t dt\), which means \(dt = \frac{dv}{v}\). When \(t = 0\), \(v = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(v \to \infty\). So the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \sin(v) \cdot \frac{dv}{v} = \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] Again, this is another sine integral, but from 1 to infinity. The integral of \(\frac{\sin v}{v}\) from 1 to infinity is equal to \(\frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1)\), since the total integral from 0 to infinity is \(\frac{\pi}{2}\). Therefore: \[ \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv = \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \] So putting both integrals together, we have: Original integral = First integral + Second integral = \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Simplifying this, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out: = \( \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Wait, that seems too straightforward. Let me verify that. Yes, because: First integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) \) Second integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Adding them together: \( \frac{\pi}{4} - \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Yes, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel each other out. Therefore, the value of the original integral is \(\frac{\pi}{4}\). But let me check once again to make sure I didn't make a mistake in substitution steps. Starting from the substitution \(x = \sinh t\), leading to the integral in terms of \(t\), which then splits into two parts. Each substitution for the two integrals seems correct. The integral of \(\sin(e^{-t})\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin u / u\) from 0 to 1, and the integral of \(\sin(e^t)\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 1 to infinity. Then, since the total integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 0 to infinity is \(\pi/2\), the part from 1 to infinity is \(\pi/2 - \text{Si}(1)\), and the part from 0 to 1 is \(\text{Si}(1)\). Then adding the two integrals multiplied by 1/2 cancels the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms. Hence, the result is indeed \(\pi/4\). That seems correct. Therefore, the value of the integral is \(\pi/4\). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} </think> To evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \), we start by using the substitution \( x = \sinh t \). This transforms the integral into: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Using the trigonometric identity \( \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \) with \( A = \sinh t \) and \( B = \cosh t \), we rewrite the integrand as: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] This splits the integral into two parts: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) For the first integral, substitute \( v = e^t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right) \). For the second integral, substitute \( u = e^{-t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \). Combining these results, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \] Thus, the value of the integral is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} \]

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Using the substitution \(x = \tan \theta\), \( \mathrm{d} x = \sec^{2} \theta\, \mathrm{d} \theta \), we have \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x = \int_{0}^{+\pi/4} \frac{\sin \theta \sin \theta}{\cos \theta} \frac{\sec^{2} \theta}{\cos \theta} \mathrm{~d} \theta = \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\pi/4} \mathrm{~d} \theta = \frac{\pi}{8} \). \boxed{\frac{\pi}{8}}

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Use \(\sin^{2} x\) instead of \(\left(\sin x\right)^{2}\) so it will work: \[ \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^2}}{\sqrt{1+x^2}} \mathrm{~d} x = \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}} \ \bigg|_0^{+\infty } = \tan^{-1} \frac{x}{1+x^2}\ \bigg|_0^{+\infty } = \frac{\pi}{2} - 0 = \frac{\pi}{2} \] \boxed{\frac{\pi}{2}} Commentor: \(\frac{\pi}{2}\) looks like a reasonable estimate, but proving it works usually takes a while. Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We use the sine integral as follows: \[\int \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d}x = \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}} (\cos \sqrt{1+x^{2}}+ \sqrt{2} \int{ \sqrt{1+x^{2}} \cos x \mathrm{~d}x})+ C\] Multiplying out the cosine integral and using the identity \(2\sin^2 \alpha =1+ \cos \, 2 \alpha\) results in \(\boxed{ \frac{\pi}{2} - \pi \tan^{-1} \sqrt{1+x^{2}}- \sqrt{2} \int \cos x \sqrt{1+x^{2}}(1+ \cos 2 x) \mathrm{~d}x } \). We see that \(2x \cos x \sin x = \sin 2x\). Therefore, we have \(\boxed{ \frac{\pi}{2} - \pi \tan^{-1} \sqrt{1+x^{2}}-2 \sqrt{2} \int (\sqrt{x^{2}+1+ \sin x} + \sqrt{2}\sin x)(\sin x) \mathrm{~d}x } \). Margin Quiz Student: Michael X. Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this improper integral: \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \). Hmm, let's see. First, this looks a bit intimidating because of the combination of cosine and sine functions with the square root in the denominator. But maybe there's a substitution or a trick that can simplify this. Let me start by analyzing the integrand. The denominator is \(\sqrt{1 + x^2}\), which reminds me of hyperbolic identities because if I set \(x = \sinh t\), then \(\sqrt{1 + x^2} = \cosh t\), and \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\). Maybe that substitution would help here. Let me try that. Let’s set \(x = \sinh t\). Then, \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\), and when \(x = 0\), \(t = 0\), and as \(x \to \infty\), \(t \to \infty\). Substituting into the integral, the denominator becomes \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), so the denominator cancels out with the \(\cosh t\) from the substitution. That simplifies the denominator part. So the integral becomes: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\cos(\sinh t) \sin(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t})}{\cosh t} \cdot \cosh t \, dt = \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Wait, because \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), right? So the sine term becomes \(\sin(\cosh t)\). So the integral simplifies to: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Hmm, that seems a bit simpler, but still not straightforward. Maybe there's a trigonometric identity I can use here. The product of cosine and sine can be written as a sum of sines: \[ \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \] Let me apply that identity here with \(A = \sinh t\) and \(B = \cosh t\). Then: \[ \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(\cosh t + \sinh t) + \sin(\cosh t - \sinh t)] \] Now, \(\cosh t + \sinh t = e^t\) because \(\cosh t = \frac{e^t + e^{-t}}{2}\) and \(\sinh t = \frac{e^t - e^{-t}}{2}\), so adding them gives \(e^t\). Similarly, \(\cosh t - \sinh t = e^{-t}\). So the expression becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \, dt \] So now, I need to evaluate two integrals: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) Let me handle them one by one. Starting with the second integral because it might be simpler. Let’s make a substitution for the second integral: Let \(u = e^{-t}\). Then, \(du = -e^{-t} dt\), which implies \(dt = -\frac{du}{u}\). When \(t = 0\), \(u = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(u \to 0\). Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{0} \sin(u) \left(-\frac{du}{u}\right) = \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] Oh, that's a familiar integral. The integral of \(\frac{\sin u}{u}\) from 0 to 1 is known as the sine integral function, denoted \(\text{Si}(1)\). But in general, \(\int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du\) doesn't have an elementary closed-form, but maybe we can leave it as \(\text{Si}(1)\) for now. Wait, but perhaps when combined with the first integral, something cancels or simplifies. Now, let's look at the first integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \). Let me make a substitution here as well. Let \(v = e^t\), so that \(dv = e^t dt\), which means \(dt = \frac{dv}{v}\). When \(t = 0\), \(v = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(v \to \infty\). So the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \sin(v) \cdot \frac{dv}{v} = \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] Again, this is another sine integral, but from 1 to infinity. The integral of \(\frac{\sin v}{v}\) from 1 to infinity is equal to \(\frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1)\), since the total integral from 0 to infinity is \(\frac{\pi}{2}\). Therefore: \[ \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv = \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \] So putting both integrals together, we have: Original integral = First integral + Second integral = \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Simplifying this, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out: = \( \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Wait, that seems too straightforward. Let me verify that. Yes, because: First integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) \) Second integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Adding them together: \( \frac{\pi}{4} - \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Yes, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel each other out. Therefore, the value of the original integral is \(\frac{\pi}{4}\). But let me check once again to make sure I didn't make a mistake in substitution steps. Starting from the substitution \(x = \sinh t\), leading to the integral in terms of \(t\), which then splits into two parts. Each substitution for the two integrals seems correct. The integral of \(\sin(e^{-t})\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin u / u\) from 0 to 1, and the integral of \(\sin(e^t)\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 1 to infinity. Then, since the total integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 0 to infinity is \(\pi/2\), the part from 1 to infinity is \(\pi/2 - \text{Si}(1)\), and the part from 0 to 1 is \(\text{Si}(1)\). Then adding the two integrals multiplied by 1/2 cancels the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms. Hence, the result is indeed \(\pi/4\). That seems correct. Therefore, the value of the integral is \(\pi/4\). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} </think> To evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \), we start by using the substitution \( x = \sinh t \). This transforms the integral into: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Using the trigonometric identity \( \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \) with \( A = \sinh t \) and \( B = \cosh t \), we rewrite the integrand as: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] This splits the integral into two parts: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) For the first integral, substitute \( v = e^t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right) \). This transforms the integral into: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Using the trigonometric identity \( \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \) with \( A = \sinh t \) and \( B = \cosh t \), we rewrite the integrand as: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] This splits the integral into two parts: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) For the first integral, substitute \( v = e^t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right) \). Combining these results, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \] Thus, the value of the integral is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} \]

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Upper limit is \infty, so the improper integral equals \( \lim_{b \to +\infty} \int_{0}^{b} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}}\mathrm{~d}x \). We have \(\int_{0}^{b} \cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}} \, \mathrm{~d}x = \int_{0}^{b} \sin(\sqrt{1+ x^{2}}+x). \, \mathrm{~d}x \)= 0; a periodic function with \(\pi\)-periodicity quantity. Therefore, \( \lim_{b \to +\infty} \int_{0}^{b} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}}\mathrm{~d}x = 0 \).\boxed{0}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this improper integral: \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \). Hmm, let's see. First, this looks a bit intimidating because of the combination of cosine and sine functions with the square root in the denominator. Maybe that substitution would help here. Let me try that. Let’s set \(x = \sinh t\). Then, \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\), and when \(x = 0\), \(t = 0\), and as \(x \to \infty\), \(t \to \infty\). Substituting into the integral, the denominator becomes \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), so the denominator cancels out with the \(\cosh t\) from the substitution. So the sine term becomes \(\sin(\cosh t)\). So the integral simplifies to: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Hmm, that seems a bit simpler, but still not straightforward. Then: \[ \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(\cosh t + \sinh t) + \sin(\cosh t - \sinh t)] \] Now, \(\cosh t + \sinh t = e^t\) because \(\cosh t = \frac{e^t + e^{-t}}{2}\) and \(\sinh t = \frac{e^t - e^{-t}}{2}\), so adding them gives \(e^t\). Starting with the second integral because it might be simpler. Let’s make a substitution for the second integral: Let \(u = e^{-t}\). Then, \(du = -e^{-t} dt\), which implies \(dt = -\frac{du}{u}\). Combining these results, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \] Thus, the value of the integral is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} \]

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \(\frac{\pi}{4} \). \boxed{\frac{\pi}{4}}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this improper integral: \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \). Hmm, let's see. First, this looks a bit intimidating because of the combination of cosine and sine functions with the square root in the denominator. But maybe there's a substitution or a trick that can simplify this. Let me start by analyzing the integrand. The denominator is \(\sqrt{1 + x^2}\), which reminds me of hyperbolic identities because if I set \(x = \sinh t\), then \(\sqrt{1 + x^2} = \cosh t\), and \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\). Maybe that substitution would help here. Let me try that. Let’s set \(x = \sinh t\). Then, \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\), and when \(x = 0\), \(t = 0\), and as \(x \to \infty\), \(t \to \infty\). Substituting into the integral, the denominator becomes \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), so the denominator cancels out with the \(\cosh t\) from the substitution. That simplifies the denominator part. So the integral becomes: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\cos(\sinh t) \sin(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t})}{\cosh t} \cdot \cosh t \, dt = \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Wait, because \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), right? So the sine term becomes \(\sin(\cosh t)\). So the integral simplifies to: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Hmm, that seems a bit simpler, but still not straightforward. Maybe there's a trigonometric identity I can use here. The product of cosine and sine can be written as a sum of sines: \[ \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \] Let me apply that identity here with \(A = \sinh t\) and \(B = \cosh t\). Then: \[ \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(\cosh t + \sinh t) + \sin(\cosh t - \sinh t)] \] Now, \(\cosh t + \sinh t = e^t\) because \(\cosh t = \frac{e^t + e^{-t}}{2}\) and \(\sinh t = \frac{e^t - e^{-t}}{2}\), so adding them gives \(e^t\). Similarly, \(\cosh t - \sinh t = e^{-t}\). So the expression becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \, dt \] So now, I need to evaluate two integrals: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) Let me handle them one by one. Starting with the second integral because it might be simpler. Let’s make a substitution for the second integral: Let \(u = e^{-t}\). Then, \(du = -e^{-t} dt\), which implies \(dt = -\frac{du}{u}\). When \(t = 0\), \(u = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(u \to 0\). Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{0} \sin(u) \left(-\frac{du}{u}\right) = \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] Oh, that's a familiar integral. The integral of \(\frac{\sin u}{u}\) from 0 to 1 is known as the sine integral function, denoted \(\text{Si}(1)\). But in general, \(\int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du\) doesn't have an elementary closed-form, but maybe we can leave it as \(\text{Si}(1)\) for now. Wait, but perhaps when combined with the first integral, something cancels or simplifies. Now, let's look at the first integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \). Let me make a substitution here as well. Let \(v = e^t\), so that \(dv = e^t dt\), which means \(dt = \frac{dv}{v}\). When \(t = 0\), \(v = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(v \to \infty\). So the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \sin(v) \cdot \frac{dv}{v} = \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] Again, this is another sine integral, but from 1 to infinity. The integral of \(\frac{\sin v}{v}\) from 1 to infinity is equal to \(\frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1)\), since the total integral from 0 to infinity is \(\frac{\pi}{2}\). Therefore: \[ \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv = \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \] So putting both integrals together, we have: Original integral = First integral + Second integral = \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Simplifying this, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out: = \( \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Wait, that seems too straightforward. Let me verify that. Yes, because: First integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) \) Second integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Adding them together: \( \frac{\pi}{4} - \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Yes, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel each other out. Therefore, the value of the original integral is \(\frac{\pi}{4}\). But let me check once again to make sure I didn't make a mistake in substitution steps. Starting from the substitution \(x = \sinh t\), leading to the integral in terms of \(t\), which then splits into two parts. Each substitution for the two integrals seems correct. The integral of \(\sin(e^{-t})\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin u / u\) from 0 to 1, and the integral of \(\sin(e^t)\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 1 to infinity. Then, since the total integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 0 to infinity is \(\pi/2\), the part from 1 to infinity is \(\pi/2 - \text{Si}(1)\), and the part from 0 to 1 is \(\text{Si}(1)\). Then adding the two integrals multiplied by 1/2 cancels the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms. Hence, the result is indeed \(\pi/4\). That seems correct. Therefore, the value of the integral is \(\pi/4\). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} </think> To evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \), we start by using the substitution \( x = \sinh t \). Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \(\int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \\ = \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin x}{x} \mathrm{~d} x = \lim_{B \rightarrow \infty} \int_{0}^{B} \frac{\cos x \sin x}{x} \mathrm{~d} x \\ = \lim_{B \rightarrow \infty} \bigg[ \cos x \ln \left| \frac{1}{x} \right| \bigg]_{0}^{B} - \sin x \tan^{-1} \left( \frac{1}{x} \right)_{0}^{B} \\ = 0 + \left( \frac{1}{B} - 0 \right) - 0 = \boxed{1}\)

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Proper terms are cos x dx and sqrt{1 + x^2} dx; the improper is from \(\infty\) to 0. We integrate by nosag. Set u = 1 + x^2. du = 2x dx. \frac{1}{2} \cosh^{-1} sqrt{u} du from 1 to 0 is -pi over 2. We have negative cos x' sin sqrt{1 + x^2} dx. \boxed{-pi/2}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this improper integral: \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \). Hmm, let's see. First, this looks a bit intimidating because of the combination of cosine and sine functions with the square root in the denominator. But maybe there's a substitution or a trick that can simplify this. Let me start by analyzing the integrand. The denominator is \(\sqrt{1 + x^2}\), which reminds me of hyperbolic identities because if I set \(x = \sinh t\), then \(\sqrt{1 + x^2} = \cosh t\), and \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\). Maybe that substitution would help here. Let me try that. Let’s set \(x = \sinh t\). Then, \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\), and when \(x = 0\), \(t = 0\), and as \(x \to \infty\), \(t \to \infty\). Substituting into the integral, the denominator becomes \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), so the denominator cancels out with the \(\cosh t\) from the substitution. That simplifies the denominator part. So the integral becomes: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\cos(\sinh t) \sin(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t})}{\cosh t} \cdot \cosh t \, dt = \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Wait, because \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), right? So the sine term becomes \(\sin(\cosh t)\). So the integral simplifies to: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Hmm, that seems a bit simpler, but still not straightforward. Maybe there's a trigonometric identity I can use here. The product of cosine and sine can be written as a sum of sines: \[ \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \] Let me apply that identity here with \(A = \sinh t\) and \(B = \cosh t\). Then: \[ \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(\cosh t + \sinh t) + \sin(\cosh t - \sinh t)] \] Now, \(\cosh t + \sinh t = e^t\) because \(\cosh t = \frac{e^t + e^{-t}}{2}\) and \(\sinh t = \frac{e^t - e^{-t}}{2}\), so adding them gives \(e^t\). Similarly, \(\cosh t - \sinh t = e^{-t}\). So the expression becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \, dt \] So now, I need to evaluate two integrals: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) Let me handle them one by one. Starting with the second integral because it might be simpler. Let’s make a substitution for the second integral: Let \(u = e^{-t}\). Then, \(du = -e^{-t} dt\), which implies \(dt = -\frac{du}{u}\). When \(t = 0\), \(u = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(u \to 0\). Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{0} \sin(u) \left(-\frac{du}{u}\right) = \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] Oh, that's a familiar integral. The integral of \(\frac{\sin u}{u}\) from 0 to 1 is known as the sine integral function, denoted \(\text{Si}(1)\). But in general, \(\int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du\) doesn't have an elementary closed-form, but maybe we can leave it as \(\text{Si}(1)\) for now. Wait, but perhaps when combined with the first integral, something cancels or simplifies. Now, let's look at the first integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \). Let me make a substitution here as well. Let \(v = e^t\), so that \(dv = e^t dt\), which means \(dt = \frac{dv}{v}\). When \(t = 0\), \(v = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(v \to \infty\). So the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \sin(v) \cdot \frac{dv}{v} = \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] Again, this is another sine integral, but from 1 to infinity. The integral of \(\frac{\sin v}{v}\) from 1 to infinity is equal to \(\frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1)\), since the total integral from 0 to infinity is \(\frac{\pi}{2}\). Therefore: \[ \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv = \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \] So putting both integrals together, we have: Original integral = First integral + Second integral = \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Simplifying this, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out: = \( \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Wait, that seems too straightforward. Let me verify that. Yes, because: First integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) \) Second integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Adding them together: \( \frac{\pi}{4} - \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Yes, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel each other out. Therefore, the value of the original integral is \(\frac{\pi}{4}\). But let me check once again to make sure I didn't make a mistake in substitution steps. Starting from the substitution \(x = \sinh t\), leading to the integral in terms of \(t\), which then splits into two parts. Each substitution for the two integrals seems correct. The integral of \(\sin(e^{-t})\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin u / u\) from 0 to 1, and the integral of \(\sin(e^t)\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 1 to infinity. Then, since the total integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 0 to infinity is \(\pi/2\), the part from 1 to infinity is \(\pi/2 - \text{Si}(1)\), and the part from 0 to 1 is \(\text{Si}(1)\). Then adding the two integrals multiplied by 1/2 cancels the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms. Hence, the result is indeed \(\pi/4\). That seems correct. Therefore, the value of the integral is \(\pi/4\). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} </think> To evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \), we start by using the substitution \( x = \sinh t \). This transforms the integral into: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Using the trigonometric identity \( \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \) with \( A = \sinh t \) and \( B = \cosh t \), we rewrite the integrand as: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] This splits the integral into two parts: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) For the first integral, substitute \( v = e^t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right) \). For the second integral, substitute \( u = e^{-t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \). Combining these results, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \] Thus, the value of the integral is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} \]

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We write 1+x^2 as x^2+1 so the integrand becomes \cos x \sin x. We write \int_{0}^{+\infty}x^n (\cos x \sin x)^{\textrm{count of x's in the integrand}} \mathrm{~d}x = \Gamma(n+1) \int_{0}^{\infty}u^n (\cos \sqrt{u}) \mathrm{~d}u. The integral is \left [ \sin \sqrt{u} (\cos \sqrt{u})^{-1} u^{n/2} \right ]_{0}^{+\infty} = \Gamma(n+1) \frac{\Gamma\left(\frac{3+n}{2} \right)}{\Gamma(n/2)}. The denominator is 2 raised to odd integer power \Lambda(n)\left(-\frac{\log 2}{2}\right)^{\gamma(n)} \sum_{r=0}^{\infty} \left(hovard kaamz ???) where \gamma(n) is Kronecker's delta, hence \boxed{\Gamma\left(\frac{3+n}{2} \right)} \equiv \Gamma(n) = 0 when n=3 \equiv 0(hsp). The numerator is (-1)^{n/2} \frac{\Gamma\left(\frac{n+4}{2} \right)}{\Gamma(n+2)} = (-1)^{n/2}!n+2 is even so \boxed{\Gamma\left(\frac{n+4}{2} \right)t/g/n+1} \equiv 0(hsp)

Problem: Evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \mathrm{~d} x \). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We have: y - automatic differentiation mode on; ordinate for y from 0 to 1/4 pi plus infinity (2); 1/4 pi x + 1/8 pi arctan (pi x) (7); Apply the upper limit; Write entire integral, without substitution; The integral is \boxed{1/8}

Reference Answer: Okay, so I need to evaluate this improper integral: \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \). Hmm, let's see. First, this looks a bit intimidating because of the combination of cosine and sine functions with the square root in the denominator. But maybe there's a substitution or a trick that can simplify this. Let me start by analyzing the integrand. The denominator is \(\sqrt{1 + x^2}\), which reminds me of hyperbolic identities because if I set \(x = \sinh t\), then \(\sqrt{1 + x^2} = \cosh t\), and \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\). Maybe that substitution would help here. Let me try that. Let’s set \(x = \sinh t\). Then, \(dx = \cosh t \, dt\), and when \(x = 0\), \(t = 0\), and as \(x \to \infty\), \(t \to \infty\). Substituting into the integral, the denominator becomes \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), so the denominator cancels out with the \(\cosh t\) from the substitution. That simplifies the denominator part. So the integral becomes: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \frac{\cos(\sinh t) \sin(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t})}{\cosh t} \cdot \cosh t \, dt = \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Wait, because \(\sqrt{1 + \sinh^2 t} = \cosh t\), right? So the sine term becomes \(\sin(\cosh t)\). So the integral simplifies to: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Hmm, that seems a bit simpler, but still not straightforward. Maybe there's a trigonometric identity I can use here. The product of cosine and sine can be written as a sum of sines: \[ \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \] Let me apply that identity here with \(A = \sinh t\) and \(B = \cosh t\). Then: \[ \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(\cosh t + \sinh t) + \sin(\cosh t - \sinh t)] \] Now, \(\cosh t + \sinh t = e^t\) because \(\cosh t = \frac{e^t + e^{-t}}{2}\) and \(\sinh t = \frac{e^t - e^{-t}}{2}\), so adding them gives \(e^t\). Similarly, \(\cosh t - \sinh t = e^{-t}\). So the expression becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \, dt \] So now, I need to evaluate two integrals: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) Let me handle them one by one. Starting with the second integral because it might be simpler. Let’s make a substitution for the second integral: Let \(u = e^{-t}\). Then, \(du = -e^{-t} dt\), which implies \(dt = -\frac{du}{u}\). When \(t = 0\), \(u = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(u \to 0\). Therefore, the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{0} \sin(u) \left(-\frac{du}{u}\right) = \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] Oh, that's a familiar integral. The integral of \(\frac{\sin u}{u}\) from 0 to 1 is known as the sine integral function, denoted \(\text{Si}(1)\). But in general, \(\int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du\) doesn't have an elementary closed-form, but maybe we can leave it as \(\text{Si}(1)\) for now. Wait, but perhaps when combined with the first integral, something cancels or simplifies. Now, let's look at the first integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \). Let me make a substitution here as well. Let \(v = e^t\), so that \(dv = e^t dt\), which means \(dt = \frac{dv}{v}\). When \(t = 0\), \(v = 1\), and as \(t \to \infty\), \(v \to \infty\). So the integral becomes: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \sin(v) \cdot \frac{dv}{v} = \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] Again, this is another sine integral, but from 1 to infinity. The integral of \(\frac{\sin v}{v}\) from 1 to infinity is equal to \(\frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1)\), since the total integral from 0 to infinity is \(\frac{\pi}{2}\). Therefore: \[ \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv = \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \] So putting both integrals together, we have: Original integral = First integral + Second integral = \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Simplifying this, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out: = \( \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Wait, that seems too straightforward. Let me verify that. Yes, because: First integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right ) \) Second integral: \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \) Adding them together: \( \frac{\pi}{4} - \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) + \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) = \frac{\pi}{4} \) Yes, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel each other out. Therefore, the value of the original integral is \(\frac{\pi}{4}\). But let me check once again to make sure I didn't make a mistake in substitution steps. Starting from the substitution \(x = \sinh t\), leading to the integral in terms of \(t\), which then splits into two parts. Each substitution for the two integrals seems correct. The integral of \(\sin(e^{-t})\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin u / u\) from 0 to 1, and the integral of \(\sin(e^t)\) from 0 to infinity becomes the integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 1 to infinity. Then, since the total integral of \(\sin v / v\) from 0 to infinity is \(\pi/2\), the part from 1 to infinity is \(\pi/2 - \text{Si}(1)\), and the part from 0 to 1 is \(\text{Si}(1)\). Then adding the two integrals multiplied by 1/2 cancels the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms. Hence, the result is indeed \(\pi/4\). That seems correct. Therefore, the value of the integral is \(\pi/4\). **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} </think> To evaluate the improper integral \( \int_{0}^{+\infty} \frac{\cos x \sin \sqrt{1+x^{2}}}{\sqrt{1+x^{2}}} \, dx \), we start by using the substitution \( x = \sinh t \). This transforms the integral into: \[ \int_{0}^{\infty} \cos(\sinh t) \sin(\cosh t) \, dt \] Using the trigonometric identity \( \cos A \sin B = \frac{1}{2} [\sin(A + B) + \sin(B - A)] \) with \( A = \sinh t \) and \( B = \cosh t \), we rewrite the integrand as: \[ \frac{1}{2} [\sin(e^t) + \sin(e^{-t})] \] This splits the integral into two parts: 1. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^t) \, dt \) 2. \( \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{\infty} \sin(e^{-t}) \, dt \) For the first integral, substitute \( v = e^t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{1}^{\infty} \frac{\sin v}{v} \, dv \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \left( \frac{\pi}{2} - \text{Si}(1) \right) \). For the second integral, substitute \( u = e^{-t \), transforming it into: \[ \frac{1}{2} \int_{0}^{1} \frac{\sin u}{u} \, du \] This evaluates to \( \frac{1}{2} \text{Si}(1) \). Combining these results, the \(\text{Si}(1)\) terms cancel out, leaving: \[ \frac{1}{2} \cdot \frac{\pi}{2} = \frac{\pi}{4} \] Thus, the value of the integral is: \[ \boxed{\dfrac{\pi}{4}} \]

Problem: The line $y = b-x$ with $0 < b < 4$ intersects the $y$-axis at $P$ and the line $x=4$ at $S$. If the ratio of the area of triangle $QRS$ to the area of triangle $QOP$ is 9:25, what is the value of $b$? Express the answer as a decimal to the nearest tenth. [asy] draw((0,-3)--(0,5.5),Arrows); draw((4,-3.5)--(4,5),Arrows); draw((-2,0)--(6,0),Arrows); draw((-2,4.5)--(6,-3.5),Arrows); dot((0,0)); dot((2.5,0)); dot((4,0)); dot((4,-1.5)); dot((0,2.5)); label("O",(0,0),SW); label("P",(0,2.5),NE); label("Q",(2.5,0),NE); label("R",(4,0),NE); label("S",(4,-1.5),SW); label("$y$-axis",(0,5.5),N); label("$x=4$",(4,5),N); label("$x$-axis",(6,0),E); label("$y=b-x$",(6,-3.5),SE); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: $x=4$ is perpendicular to $y=b-x$ and the equation of the perpendicular meniscus line is $\frac{x}{5}+\frac{y}{1}=1$. \boxed{\sqrt{\frac{16}{25}}}<|end_of_text|>

Reference Answer: The line $y=b-x$ intersects the $x$-axis at the point where $0 = b-x$, or $x=b$. So, we seek the $x$-coordinate of point $Q$. Since the $y$-axis is parallel to the line $x = 4$, we see that $\angle QSR = \angle QPO$. Also $QOP = QRS = 90$. Thus $\triangle QOP \sim \triangle QRS$, so $\frac{[QRS]}{[QOP]} =\left(\frac{QR}{QO}\right)^2$, which means we have $\left(\frac{QR}{QO}\right)^2=\frac{9}{25}$, so $\frac{QR}{QO} = \frac35$. Since $QR + QO= 4$, we have $\frac35QO + QO = 4$, and $QO =4\cdot \frac58 = \frac52$. Therefore, the $x$-coordinate of $Q$ is $\frac52 = \boxed{2.5}$.

Format Valid: ✗, Correct: ✗, Reward: -0.10

Response: We apply the equation from question 2 to every point on $y=b-x$ to get the slope of that line as $b$ and then use the $y$-intercept to get the $y$-value expressed as a function of $x$ to find the ratio of areas. The slope_$b$_-axis_to_$x=4$is calculated by the following tables: $-\$$ \!420 \(85\$$\!100 \(25\$$\!900 \(125\$$\!2250 \(175\$$\!1400 \(1\$$\!1122 \(7\$$\!14825 \(23\$$\!9730 $$-$$\!22525 $$-$$\!234766 So the slopes are all positive and increase from bottom left to top right. The height of triangle $QRS$ is 5. Then the area of triangle $QRS$ is found by having the coordinates make up its four corners: $OPQRS=-1,0,5,7$ and its area is ${5\over2} \cdot 7$. The area of triangle $QRS$ is 22.5. Next, $OQP=-1,0,-1,1$ and its area is found by averaging the coordinates of its corners so \({1+0-1\over2}\cdot 7=5.5\), or 5.5 square units. So the area of triangle $QOP$ is 9.75 square units, and the ratio of the areas is $9\over25$ which is approximately $0.36$. Substituting this ratio and $x=4$ into the equation $\dfrac{Area\,ofQRS}{Area\,ofQOP}=9:25$, we get 0.2993. Writing this number as a decimal with two places after the decimal point, we get $0.2993$. The final answer is \boxed{0.3} \big)\* (2 pts). Assistant: Instead of using ratios of areas, we can also use coordinate distance as follows. Since the $x$-coordinates are the same for both sides, all that remains is to add and subtract the $y$-coordinates. Doing so, we get... $-1 + (-1) + 5 - 5 = 0 + 0 - 0 = 0$ $1 - 0 + 5 - (-4.5) = 1 - 0 +5 +8.5 = 1 + 13.5 = 14.5$ Doing so above, we get area 45 for triangle $QRS$ and area 0 for triangle $QOP$, leading to a ratio of 14.5:0, or a ratio of 45:0, which is the same as 45:1, or a ratio of 45:900, which is the same as $45/900=1/20$. Expressing this number as a decimal with two places after the decimal point, we get $0.05$. Substituting this ratio and $x=4$ into the equation $\dfrac{Area\,ofQRS}{Area\,ofQOP}=9:25$, we get 2.0. [asy] draw((0,-3)--(0,5.5),Arrows); draw((4,-3.5)--(4,5),Arrows); draw((-2,0)--(6,0),Arrows); draw((-2,4.5)--(6,-3.5),Arrows); dot((0,0)); dot((2.5,0)); dot((4,0)); dot((4,-1.5)); dot((0,2.5)); label("O",(0,0),SW); label("P",(0,2.5),NE); label("Q",(2.5,0),NE); label("R",(4,0),NE); label("S",(4,-1.5),SW); label("$y$-axis",(0,5.5),N); label("$x=4$",(4,5),N); label("$x$-axis",(6,0),E); label("$y=b-x$",(6,-3.5),SE); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Recall that the equation of a line is given by $y = m*x + b$, where $m$ is the slope and any number $b$ will work (i.e. $m*x+b$ can describe any point on a line.) We look for a value of $b$ that satisfies $y = b-x$ and the condition that the line must meet the $y$-axis at $P$. Then we look at $x=4$ and move 4 units to the right along the $x$-axis. We count and find 9 triangles. IE we have 9 triangles for $x=2.5$ which is 2.5 times larger. Thus, $\boxed{b=2.25}$. QED.
