Problem: The line $y = b-x$ with $0 < b < 4$ intersects the $y$-axis at $P$ and the line $x=4$ at $S$. If the ratio of the area of triangle $QRS$ to the area of triangle $QOP$ is 9:25, what is the value of $b$? Express the answer as a decimal to the nearest tenth. [asy] draw((0,-3)--(0,5.5),Arrows); draw((4,-3.5)--(4,5),Arrows); draw((-2,0)--(6,0),Arrows); draw((-2,4.5)--(6,-3.5),Arrows); dot((0,0)); dot((2.5,0)); dot((4,0)); dot((4,-1.5)); dot((0,2.5)); label("O",(0,0),SW); label("P",(0,2.5),NE); label("Q",(2.5,0),NE); label("R",(4,0),NE); label("S",(4,-1.5),SW); label("$y$-axis",(0,5.5),N); label("$x=4$",(4,5),N); label("$x$-axis",(6,0),E); label("$y=b-x$",(6,-3.5),SE); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Well, $b-x$ is just $4-y$, so line $y=4$ is $x=4-y$. The parts of this that are furthest apart are the parts at $y=4$, so those are (4,0) and (4,-1.5)=(4,0)-(4,-1.5)=(4,0)-5=RQ. For the triangle $QOS$, the height is the entire path from Q to the y-axis (which is the whole thing, since $b<4$), and the width is the distance between the $y$-axis and S (which is also 4). The area is $(4/2)*4=(4)^2/2=4^2$. Now the area of triangle $QOP$is (b/2)*(OS)=(b*4)/2=b*4, and the ratio is 4 to (4/2)=2. So \boxed{b=4}.

Thus $\triangle QOP \sim \triangle QRS$, so $\frac{[QRS]}{[QOP]} =\left(\frac{QR}{QO}\right)^2$, which means we have $\left(\frac{QR}{QO}\right)^2=\frac{9}{25}$, so $\frac{QR}{QO} = \frac35$. Since $QR + QO= 4$, we have $\frac35QO + QO = 4$, and $QO =4\cdot \frac58 = \frac52$. Therefore, the $x$-coordinate of $Q$ is $\frac52 = \boxed{2.5}$.

Reference Answer: The line $y=b-x$ intersects the $x$-axis at the point where $0 = b-x$, or $x=b$. So, we seek the $x$-coordinate of point $Q$. Since the $y$-axis is parallel to the line $x = 4$, we see that $\angle QSR = \angle QPO$. Also $QOP = QRS = 90$. Thus $\triangle QOP \sim \triangle QRS$, so $\frac{[QRS]}{[QOP]} =\left(\frac{QR}{QO}\right)^2$, which means we have $\left(\frac{QR}{QO}\right)^2=\frac{9}{25}$, so $\frac{QR}{QO} = \frac35$. Since $QR + QO= 4$, we have $\frac35QO + QO = 4$, and $QO =4\cdot \frac58 = \frac52$. Therefore, the $x$-coordinate of $Q$ is $\frac52 = \boxed{2.5}$.

Format Valid: ✓, Correct: ✗, Reward: 0.00

Problem: The line $y = b-x$ with $0 < b < 4$ intersects the $y$-axis at $P$ and the line $x=4$ at $S$. If the ratio of the area of triangle $QRS$ to the area of triangle $QOP$ is 9:25, what is the value of $b$? Express the answer as a decimal to the nearest tenth. [asy] draw((0,-3)--(0,5.5),Arrows); draw((4,-3.5)--(4,5),Arrows); draw((-2,0)--(6,0),Arrows); draw((-2,4.5)--(6,-3.5),Arrows); dot((0,0)); dot((2.5,0)); dot((4,0)); dot((4,-1.5)); dot((0,2.5)); label("O",(0,0),SW); label("P",(0,2.5),NE); label("Q",(2.5,0),NE); label("R",(4,0),NE); label("S",(4,-1.5),SW); label("$y$-axis",(0,5.5),N); label("$x=4$",(4,5),N); label("$x$-axis",(6,0),E); label("$y=b-x$",(6,-3.5),SE); [/asy] Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: I'm tempted to use my regular old "completing the square wouldn't be a lot of fun method" to solve this. That might work, but really, we should give it a shot. Some things to notice: we know that the $x$-intercept will be 0. We also know that $b=4-s$. Finally, I think the question means you take the base of the height of $SQ$ and multiply to get the area of triangle $QRS$, not the height of $SQ$ itself. But let's just number our points first. $P(0,b),Q(2.5,0),R(4,0),S(4,4-b),O(0,0)$. We can find the slope of $QOS$: given that the $x$-intercept is 0, the slope is $-1$. Therefore, by the Pythagorean Theorem, $\triangle QOS$ has base length 3.5 and height 2, so its area is 3.5*2=$7$. The height of $\triangle QRP$ is $4-b$; we have two other sides, $x=4, OP=SQ$, which is the same as $RQ$ by the Pythagorean Theorem. So $\triangle QRP$ must be a 45-45-90 triangle, with base 3.5 and height $\frac{3.5}{\sqrt{2}}$. Its area is $\frac{3.5^2}{2}=\frac{3.5}{2}$ Thus the area ratio: 7:3.5=2:1, so 9:25=3:5, so b=3.5, for a final answer of \boxed{3.5}.

Reference Answer: The line $y=b-x$ intersects the $x$-axis at the point where $0 = b-x$, or $x=b$. So, we seek the $x$-coordinate of point $Q$. Since $QR + QO= 4$, we have $\frac35QO + QO = 4$, and $QO =4\cdot \frac58 = \frac52$. Therefore, the $x$-coordinate of $Q$ is $\frac52 = \boxed{2.5}$.

Problem: Find the directional derivative of the function \( f(x,y) = \begin{cases}\frac{x^3+y^3}{x^2-y^2},\ x^2-y^2\neq 0 \\\ \,\ \,\ 0 \,\ \,\ ,x^2-y^2=0\end{cases} \) at the point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: Treat the word like a fraction. Combine terms and simplify. First, substitute \(x =-1\) and \(y =0\): \(f(-1,0) = \frac{(-1)^3 +0^3}{(-1)^2-0^2} = \frac{1}{-1} = -1 \). Using the quotient rule to handle the derivative, we have: \( f_x = \frac{3x^2 +y^3}{(x^2-y^2)^2}\). We need a partial derivative with respect to \(x\), so we differentiate the numerator and denominator as always, and set \(y\) equal to \(0\): \(f_{xy} = \frac{6x}{(x^2-0^2)^2} = \frac{6x}{x^2} = 6x\). \(f_{xx} = \frac{12}{x^2}\). Plugging into the quotient rule, first we find: \(f_{xxy} = \frac{12}{-3x^2} + \frac{3x^2}{(x^2-0^2)^3}\). Now, we use that \(\frac{f'(x,y)}{h}\) equals \(f_x +yf_{xy} +x f_{xy}\), and substitute \(x=0\): \(\frac{f'(-2/5,3/5)}{h} = \frac{-6}{5^3} + \frac{6x}{5^4} = -\frac{24}{125} + 0 =-\frac{-18}{125} = \boxed{0.72}\).

Reference Answer: Alright, so I need to find the directional derivative of this piecewise function at (0,0) in the direction of the vector ⟨4/5, 3/5⟩. Let me start by recalling what a directional derivative is. The directional derivative of a function f at a point P in the direction of a unit vector u is the rate at which f changes at P in the direction u. The formula for it is the dot product of the gradient of f at P and the unit vector u. But wait, does this function have a gradient at (0,0)? Hmm, the function is defined piecewise, with the value 0 at (0,0) since x² - y² = 0 there. For other points where x² - y² ≠ 0, it's (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²). First, I need to check if the function is differentiable at (0,0). If it isn't, then the gradient might not exist, and I can't use the standard gradient formula. Alternatively, even if the gradient doesn't exist, the directional derivative might still exist using the limit definition. The directional derivative D_u f(0,0) can be computed as the limit as h approaches 0 of [f((0,0) + h*u) - f(0,0)] / h. Since f(0,0) is 0, that simplifies to the limit as h approaches 0 of f(h*(4/5), h*(3/5)) / h. So maybe I should use this limit definition directly? Let's try that. Let me set up the limit. The directional derivative \( D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) \) in the direction of a unit vector \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is given by: \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f((0,0) + h\mathbf{u}) - f(0,0)}{h} \] Substituting \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\), we evaluate \( f \) at the point \(( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} )\): 1. **Check the denominator \( x^2 - y^2 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^2 - \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^2 = \frac{16h^2}{25} - \frac{9h^2}{25} = \frac{7h^2}{25} \neq 0 \text{ for } h \neq 0 \] 2. **Compute the numerator \( x^3 + y^3 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^3 = \frac{64h^3}{125} + \frac{27h^3}{125} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \] 3. **Evaluate \( f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) \):** \[ f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) = \frac{\frac{91h^3}{125}}{\frac{7h^2}{25}} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \times \frac{25}{7h^2} = \frac{91h \times 25}{125 \times 7} = \frac{13h}{5} \] 4. **Compute the directional derivative:** \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{\frac{13h}{5}}{h} = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{13}{5} = \frac{13}{5} \] Thus, the directional derivative of the function at \((0,0)\) in the direction of \(\langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{13}{5}}\).

Response: (In terms of x and y, the partial derivative of a function with respect to x is to be found by holding all other variables constant and taking the ordinary derivative.) The derivative of the function with respect to y is 0. The derivative of the function with respect to x is \(\frac{3}{2} x^2 +\frac{3}{2} y^2\). To find the directional derivative at point (0, 0) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\), multiply the partial derivative with respect to x at \((0, 0)\) by the first coordinate of the vector and add to the partial derivative with respect to y at \((0, 0)\) times the second coordinate of the vector. This is \(\boxed{\frac{4}{5} \frac{3}{2} +\frac{16}{5}\frac{3}{2} = 18}\).<|end_of_text|>

The directional derivative of a function f at a point P in the direction of a unit vector u is the rate at which f changes at P in the direction u. The formula for it is the dot product of the gradient of f at P and the unit vector u. But wait, does this function have a gradient at (0,0)? Hmm, the function is defined piecewise, with the value 0 at (0,0) since x² - y² = 0 there. For other points where x² - y² ≠ 0, it's (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²). First, I need to check if the function is differentiable at (0,0). If it isn't, then the gradient might not exist, and I can't use the standard gradient formula. Alternatively, even if the gradient doesn't exist, the directional derivative might still exist using the limit definition. The directional derivative D_u f(0,0) can be computed as the limit as h approaches 0 of [f((0,0) + h*u) - f(0,0)] / h. Since f(0,0) is 0, that simplifies to the limit as h approaches 0 of f(h*(4/5), h*(3/5)) / h. So maybe I should use this limit definition directly? Let's try that. Let me set up the limit. The direction vector is ⟨4/5, 3/5⟩, which is already a unit vector because (4/5)² + (3/5)² = 16/25 + 9/25 = 25/25 = 1. So I don't need to normalize it. Then, moving along this direction from (0,0), the point after a step h is (4h/5, 3h/5). Now, I need to compute f at this point and then divide by h, taking the limit as h approaches 0. So let's compute f(4h/5, 3h/5). First, check if x² - y² ≠ 0. Let's compute x² - y² at (4h/5, 3h/5): x² - y² = (4h/5)² - (3h/5)² = (16h²/25) - (9h²/25) = 7h²/25. Since h ≠ 0 (we're taking the limit as h approaches 0), this is not zero. Therefore, we use the first case of f: (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²). So compute numerator and denominator: Numerator: x³ + y³ = (4h/5)^3 + (3h/5)^3 = (64h³/125) + (27h³/125) = 91h³/125. Denominator: x² - y² = 7h²/25 as above. Therefore, f(4h/5, 3h/5) = (91h³/125) / (7h²/25) = (91h³/125) * (25/(7h²)) = (91h * 25) / (125 * 7) = (91h * 5) / (25 * 7) [because 25/125 = 1/5] Wait, maybe simplifying step by step: First, 91/7 = 13, so 91 divided by 7 is 13. Then, 25/125 = 1/5. So: (91/7)*(25/125)*h = 13*(1/5)*h = (13/5)h. Therefore, f(4h/5, 3h/5) = (13/5)h. Then, the directional derivative is the limit as h approaches 0 of [f(4h/5, 3h/5)] / h = (13/5 h) / h = 13/5. Therefore, the directional derivative is 13/5. Hmm, that seems straightforward, but let me double-check. Wait, let's verify the calculations again step by step to be sure. First, substituting x = 4h/5, y = 3h/5. Compute numerator: x³ + y³ = (4h/5)^3 + (3h/5)^3. Let's compute each term: (4h/5)^3 = 64h³ / 125 (3h/5)^3 = 27h³ / 125 Sum: 64 + 27 = 91, so numerator is 91h³ / 125. Denominator: x² - y² = (4h/5)^2 - (3h/5)^2 = 16h²/25 - 9h²/25 = 7h²/25. Thus, f(x,y) = (91h³/125) / (7h²/25) = (91/125)/(7/25) * h = (91/125)*(25/7) * h = (91*25)/(125*7) * h. Simplify 25/125 = 1/5, and 91/7 =13. So 13 * 1/5 = 13/5. Therefore, f(x,y) = (13/5)h. Then, [f(h) - f(0,0)] / h = (13/5 h - 0)/h = 13/5. So the limit as h approaches 0 is 13/5, which exists. Therefore, the directional derivative is 13/5. Wait, but I should check if there are any issues with approaching from different directions. The directional derivative \( D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) \) in the direction of a unit vector \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is given by: \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f((0,0) + h\mathbf{u}) - f(0,0)}{h} \] Substituting \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\), we evaluate \( f \) at the point \(( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} )\): 1. **Check the denominator \( x^2 - y^2 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^2 - \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^2 = \frac{16h^2}{25} - \frac{9h^2}{25} = \frac{7h^2}{25} \neq 0 \text{ for } h \neq 0 \] 2. **Compute the numerator \( x^3 + y^3 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^3 = \frac{64h^3}{125} + \frac{27h^3}{125} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \] 3. **Evaluate \( f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) \):** \[ f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) = \frac{\frac{91h^3}{125}}{\frac{7h^2}{25}} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \times \frac{25}{7h^2} = \frac{91h \times 25}{125 \times 7} = \frac{13h}{5} \] 4. **Compute the directional derivative:** \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{\frac{13h}{5}}{h} = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{13}{5} = \frac{13}{5} \] Thus, the directional derivative of the function at \((0,0)\) in the direction of \(\langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{13}{5}}\).

In the limit when \(h\rightarrow 0\), we see that \(h^2\rightarrow 0\), giving us \( f_x(0,0) = -5\). Similarly \( f_y(0,0) = 3\). Therefore \(\boxed{\nabla f(0,0)=\langle-5,&3\rangle} \)

Problem: Find the directional derivative of the function \( f(x,y) = \begin{cases}\frac{x^3+y^3}{x^2-y^2},\ x^2-y^2\neq 0 \\\ \,\ \,\ 0 \,\ \,\ ,x^2-y^2=0\end{cases} \) at the point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: We need to evaluate the directional derivative at point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5}\rangle\). We need to find the angle the vector makes with the positive \(x\)-axis. \(\frac{4}{5}\sec(\theta)=0\). This means that \(\theta = \frac{3}{4}\pi\). The directional derivative at point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5}\rangle\) is \(\boxed{10}\).

Reference Answer: Alright, so I need to find the directional derivative of this piecewise function at (0,0) in the direction of the vector ⟨4/5, 3/5⟩. Let me start by recalling what a directional derivative is. The directional derivative of a function f at a point P in the direction of a unit vector u is the rate at which f changes at P in the direction u. The formula for it is the dot product of the gradient of f at P and the unit vector u. But wait, does this function have a gradient at (0,0)? Hmm, the function is defined piecewise, with the value 0 at (0,0) since x² - y² = 0 there. For other points where x² - y² ≠ 0, it's (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²). First, I need to check if the function is differentiable at (0,0). If it isn't, then the gradient might not exist, and I can't use the standard gradient formula. Alternatively, even if the gradient doesn't exist, the directional derivative might still exist using the limit definition. The directional derivative D_u f(0,0) can be computed as the limit as h approaches 0 of [f((0,0) + h*u) - f(0,0)] / h. Since f(0,0) is 0, that simplifies to the limit as h approaches 0 of f(h*(4/5), h*(3/5)) / h. So maybe I should use this limit definition directly? Let's try that. Let me set up the limit. Then, moving along this direction from (0,0), the point after a step h is (4h/5, 3h/5). Now, I need to compute f at this point and then divide by h, taking the limit as h approaches 0. So let's compute f(4h/5, 3h/5). First, check if x² - y² ≠ 0. Let's compute x² - y² at (4h/5, 3h/5): x² - y² = (4h/5)² - (3h/5)² = (16h²/25) - (9h²/25) = 7h²/25. Since h ≠ 0 (we're taking the limit as h approaches 0), this is not zero. Therefore, we use the first case of f: (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²). Therefore, f(4h/5, 3h/5) = (91h³/125) / (7h²/25) = (91h³/125) * (25/(7h²)) = (91h * 25) / (125 * 7) = (91h * 5) / (25 * 7) [because 25/125 = 1/5] Wait, maybe simplifying step by step: First, 91/7 = 13, so 91 divided by 7 is 13. Then, the directional derivative is the limit as h approaches 0 of [f(4h/5, 3h/5)] / h = (13/5 h) / h = 13/5. Therefore, the directional derivative is 13/5. Compute numerator: x³ + y³ = (4h/5)^3 + (3h/5)^3. Let's compute each term: (4h/5)^3 = 64h³ / 125 (3h/5)^3 = 27h³ / 125 Sum: 64 + 27 = 91, so numerator is 91h³ / 125. Denominator: x² - y² = (4h/5)^2 - (3h/5)^2 = 16h²/25 - 9h²/25 = 7h²/25. Thus, f(x,y) = (91h³/125) / (7h²/25) = (91/125)/(7/25) * h = (91/125)*(25/7) * h = (91*25)/(125*7) * h. Simplify 25/125 = 1/5, and 91/7 =13. So 13 * 1/5 = 13/5. Therefore, f(x,y) = (13/5)h. Then, [f(h) - f(0,0)] / h = (13/5 h - 0)/h = 13/5. So the limit as h approaches 0 is 13/5, which exists. The directional derivative \( D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) \) in the direction of a unit vector \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is given by: \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f((0,0) + h\mathbf{u}) - f(0,0)}{h} \] Substituting \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\), we evaluate \( f \) at the point \(( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} )\): 1. **Check the denominator \( x^2 - y^2 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^2 - \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^2 = \frac{16h^2}{25} - \frac{9h^2}{25} = \frac{7h^2}{25} \neq 0 \text{ for } h \neq 0 \] 2. **Compute the numerator \( x^3 + y^3 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^3 = \frac{64h^3}{125} + \frac{27h^3}{125} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \] 3. **Evaluate \( f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) \):** \[ f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) = \frac{\frac{91h^3}{125}}{\frac{7h^2}{25}} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \times \frac{25}{7h^2} = \frac{91h \times 25}{125 \times 7} = \frac{13h}{5} \] 4. **Compute the directional derivative:** \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{\frac{13h}{5}}{h} = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{13}{5} = \frac{13}{5} \] Thus, the directional derivative of the function at \((0,0)\) in the direction of \(\langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{13}{5}}\).

Problem: Find the directional derivative of the function \( f(x,y) = \begin{cases}\frac{x^3+y^3}{x^2-y^2},\ x^2-y^2\neq 0 \\\ \,\ \,\ 0 \,\ \,\ ,x^2-y^2=0\end{cases} \) at the point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: \[\begin{align}f_x&=\frac32\frac{x^3+y^3}{(x^2-y^2)^2}(2x)-\frac12[(x^2-y^2)]^{-2}(x^2-2x(0)y+2y^2)(2)\\&=\frac32\frac{x^3+y^3}{(x^2-y^2)^2}+2\frac{0+y^3}{(x^2-y^2)^2}=0+x=\frac14\\f_y&=\frac{\frac{3x^3}{x^2-y^2}+3yx^2}{(x^2-y^2)^2}=y+12xy\end{align}\] The directional derivative is \(u(-x-y)+v(f_x+f_y)=UTE(u(x^2-y^2)2xy)\) \(=\frac14\times(\frac43-\frac37)=\frac{3\times2}{5\times2}=\boxed{\frac{3}{10}}\)

Problem: Find the directional derivative of the function \( f(x,y) = \begin{cases}\frac{x^3+y^3}{x^2-y^2},\ x^2-y^2\neq 0 \\\ \,\ \,\ 0 \,\ \,\ ,x^2-y^2=0\end{cases} \) at the point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: First, write the functional and directional derivative with the given point and direction: \begin{align} D_ud\langle 4/5,\frac{3}{5} \rangle f(0,0) &= \frac{df(0,0)\cdot d\langle 4/5,\frac{3}{5}\rangle}{||d\langle 4/5,\frac{3}{5} \rangle||}~. \end{align} Then compute the directional derivative: \begin{align}\displaystyle D_ud\langle 4/5,\frac{3}{5} \rangle f(0,0) &= \frac{\frac{5}{2}(0+8)\cdot \frac{4}{5}+\frac{2}{5}(0+12)\cdot \frac{3}{5}}{\sqrt{5^2+3^2}} \\ &= \frac{40}{13}. \boxed{\frac{40}{13}} \end{align} The directional derivative is \boxed{\frac{40}{13}}.

The directional derivative \( D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) \) in the direction of a unit vector \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is given by: \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f((0,0) + h\mathbf{u}) - f(0,0)}{h} \] Substituting \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\), we evaluate \( f \) at the point \(( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} )\): 1. **Check the denominator \( x^2 - y^2 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^2 - \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^2 = \frac{16h^2}{25} - \frac{9h^2}{25} = \frac{7h^2}{25} \neq 0 \text{ for } h \neq 0 \] 2. **Compute the numerator \( x^3 + y^3 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^3 = \frac{64h^3}{125} + \frac{27h^3}{125} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \] 3. **Evaluate \( f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) \):** \[ f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) = \frac{\frac{91h^3}{125}}{\frac{7h^2}{25}} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \times \frac{25}{7h^2} = \frac{91h \times 25}{125 \times 7} = \frac{13h}{5} \] 4. **Compute the directional derivative:** \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{\frac{13h}{5}}{h} = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{13}{5} = \frac{13}{5} \] Thus, the directional derivative of the function at \((0,0)\) in the direction of \(\langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{13}{5}}\).

Problem: Find the directional derivative of the function \( f(x,y) = \begin{cases}\frac{x^3+y^3}{x^2-y^2},\ x^2-y^2\neq 0 \\\ \,\ \,\ 0 \,\ \,\ ,x^2-y^2=0\end{cases} \) at the point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\). Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Write your answer in \boxed{} format.

Response: First, we use the definition of the directional derivative, and substitute in the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\). This directional derivative at the point \((0,0)\) is equal to \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle f_{x}(0,0) + \langle\frac{3}{5},-\frac{4}{5}\rangle f_{y}(0,0).\) Since \(f\) is a composite function, we evaluate each part, just as we did in the "Bunny" problem and in the divergence theorem. Here, \(f_x(0,0)=\frac{3}{2},f_y(0,0)=0.\) Thus, our directional derivative is equal to \(\frac{4}{5} \cdot (\frac{3}{2}) + \frac{3}{5} \cdot (0) = \boxed{\frac{6}{5}}\)

Wait, no, actually, (91/125)*(25/7) = (91/7)*(25/125) = 13*(1/5) = 13/5. Therefore, it's 13/5 * t. Then, [f(t) - f(0)] / t = (13/5 t - 0)/t = 13/5. So same result. So yes, the directional derivative is 13/5. Alternatively, if I use the definition of directional derivative: D_u f(0,0) = lim_{h→0} [f(0 + h*(4/5), 0 + h*(3/5)) - f(0,0)] / h. Which is exactly what I computed. So this seems consistent. Wait, but another thought: since the function is defined as 0 at (0,0), which is on the line x² - y² =0, which at (0,0) is just the origin. But when approaching along the direction (4/5, 3/5), the path x=4h/5, y=3h/5 is such that x² - y² =7h²/25 ≠0 for h≠0, so we are approaching along a path that stays in the region where f is defined by (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²). Therefore, the calculation is valid. If we had approached along a direction where x² - y² =0, like along y = x or y = -x, then we would have different behavior. But here, the direction is such that x² - y² ≠0 along that path (except at h=0). Therefore, the limit only involves the expression (x³ + y³)/(x² - y²), which simplifies to 13/5 as h approaches 0. Therefore, I think the answer is 13/5. Let me check if I made a mistake in simplifying fractions. Let's go through the computation again: Numerator: x³ + y³ = (4h/5)^3 + (3h/5)^3 = 64h³/125 + 27h³/125 = 91h³/125. Denominator: x² - y² = (16h²/25 -9h²/25) =7h²/25. Therefore, f(x,y) = (91h³/125) / (7h²/25) = (91/7)*(h³/h²)*(25/125) =13*(h)*(1/5)=13h/5. Then, [f(x,y) - 0]/h = (13h/5)/h =13/5. Yep, that's consistent. So, even after multiple checks, the answer seems to be 13/5. Therefore, the directional derivative at (0,0) in the direction ⟨4/5,3/5⟩ is 13/5. **Final Answer** \boxed{\dfrac{13}{5}} </think> To find the directional derivative of the function \( f(x,y) = \begin{cases}\frac{x^3+y^3}{x^2-y^2},\ x^2-y^2\neq 0 \\ 0, \ x^2-y^2=0\end{cases} \) at the point \((0,0)\) in the direction of the vector \(\langle\frac{4}{5},\frac{3}{5} \rangle\), we use the limit definition of the directional derivative. The directional derivative \( D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) \) in the direction of a unit vector \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is given by: \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{f((0,0) + h\mathbf{u}) - f(0,0)}{h} \] Substituting \(\mathbf{u} = \langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\), we evaluate \( f \) at the point \(( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} )\): 1. **Check the denominator \( x^2 - y^2 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^2 - \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^2 = \frac{16h^2}{25} - \frac{9h^2}{25} = \frac{7h^2}{25} \neq 0 \text{ for } h \neq 0 \] 2. **Compute the numerator \( x^3 + y^3 \):** \[ \left( \frac{4h}{5} \right)^3 + \left( \frac{3h}{5} \right)^3 = \frac{64h^3}{125} + \frac{27h^3}{125} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \] 3. **Evaluate \( f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) \):** \[ f \left( \frac{4h}{5}, \frac{3h}{5} \right) = \frac{\frac{91h^3}{125}}{\frac{7h^2}{25}} = \frac{91h^3}{125} \times \frac{25}{7h^2} = \frac{91h \times 25}{125 \times 7} = \frac{13h}{5} \] 4. **Compute the directional derivative:** \[ D_{\mathbf{u}} f(0,0) = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{\frac{13h}{5}}{h} = \lim_{h \to 0} \frac{13}{5} = \frac{13}{5} \] Thus, the directional derivative of the function at \((0,0)\) in the direction of \(\langle \frac{4}{5}, \frac{3}{5} \rangle\) is \(\boxed{\dfrac{13}{5}}\).
